33	    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor):
34	        values, indices = torch.topk(x, k=self.k, dim=-1, largest=True, sorted=True)
35	        return values, indices

import torch
import reference
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')

for shape in shapes.SHAPES:
    reference.batch = shape['batch']
    reference.n = shape['n']
    reference.k = shape['k']
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    x = torch.randn(shape['batch'], shape['n'], dtype=torch.float32, device=device)
    
    # Warm up
    for _ in range(10):
        model(x)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    # Time
    start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    start.record()
    for _ in range(100):
        model(x)
    end.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 100
    gbps = (shape['batch'] * shape['n'] * 4 + shape['batch'] * shape['k'] * 12) / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e9
    print(f'Shape {shape}: {ms:.4f} ms, {gbps:.1f} GB/s')

reasoning (37481 chars)
Key specs: - RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell: 188 SMs, 1.8 TB/s DRAM bandwidth, 96 GB VRAM - SM 12.0 (Blackwell), 1536 max threads per SM, 1024 max threads per block - Triton 3.6.0 available - PyTorch 2.11.0, CUDA 13.0 No CUTLASS available. I need to build a custom top-k kernel. Let me think about the approach: The key insight: torch.topk on PyTorch dispatches to either a radix-select kernel or a bitonic sort kernel depending on size. For the shapes we have, especially the (1, 131072, 64) shape, torch.topk is getting only ~12.7 GB/s, which is terrible compared to 1800 GB/s peak. There's massive room for improvement. Let me think about the strategy: **Shape analysis:** 1. `batch=1, n=131072, k=64`: This is a single long vector. Dominated by the input read (131K fp32 = 512 KB). With 1.8 TB/s, reading 512 KB should take ~0.3 μs, but we're spending 41 μs. We need to saturate memory bandwidth. 2. `batch=64, n=8192, k=8`: 64 rows of 8K elements each. 64 * 8192 * 4 = 2 MB input. 3. `batch=32, n=16384, k=32`: 2 MB input. 4. `batch=16, n=12000, k=16`: 16 * 12000 * 4 = 768 KB input. 5. `batch=128, n=4096, k=1`: argmax, ~2 MB input. These are all very small inputs - the top-k is extremely memory-bound. The challenge is to get high memory bandwidth utilization on small inputs. **Approaches:** 1. **Bitonic Sort**: Classic approach for top-k. Sort the whole array and take the first k elements. The bitonic network does O(n log²n) comparisons, but since we only need top-k, we can use a partial bitonic sort. For k << n, this gives us O(n log k) comparisons. 2. **Radix Select**: Like bucket sort but we only need the top bucket. This can be very efficient for floating point, using the bit representation directly. 3. **Triton implementation**: Write in Triton for portability and good performance on Blackwell. Let me think about the most promising approach... For the (1, 131072, 64) shape, the input is a single large vector. We want to read it as fast as possible and find the top 64 elements. **Key algorithmic insight**: For k much smaller than n, we should use a streaming partial-sort approach rather than sorting everything. Let me think about what's most efficient: **Approach: Triton-based bitonic top-k** For each row: - Pad n to next power of 2 - Run a bitonic sort network - Take top k But wait - bitonic sort requires O(n log²n) work, which is a lot of operations (albeit simple compare-and-swap). For n=131K, log²n ≈ 17² = 289. Each comparison is a single cmp+swap. So ~131K * 289 / 2 ≈ 19 million comparisons. That's actually tiny in terms of floating point operations. But the challenge is memory access pattern - bitonic sort has terrible memory locality if done naively. **Better approach: Register-based partial sort for small k** For k=1..64, we can maintain a heap or sorted list of the top-k values and their indices as we stream through the input. This is O(n * k) register operations but only O(n) memory reads, which is optimal for memory bandwidth. For each element, we compare against the k-th largest seen so far. If larger, we insert it into our top-k list (binary search + shift). Since k is small (max 64), this insertion is cheap. This seems like the best approach for the memory-bound case. The only issue is that the per-element work scales with k, but k is at most 64. **Actually, let me reconsider.** For small k, a streaming top-k with a sorted buffer in registers is very efficient. For larger k, we might want a different approach. Let me design the Triton kernel: ``` For each program (handling a batch element): 1. Initialize a sorted buffer of k pairs (value, index) 2. For each block of input elements: a. Load a tile into shared memory or registers b. For each element, compare against k-th largest, insert if larger 3. Write out the sorted top-k ``` The challenge is that k is small (max 64) but n can be large (131K). So each thread block needs to process many elements. For the batch=1, n=131072 case, we could parallelize across the n dimension. But that complicates the merge step. What if we use a radix-based approach for fp32? **Radix select for fp32 top-k:** fp32 numbers have the nice property that sorting by their bit pattern (as uint32) gives the same ordering as sorting by value, except for the sign bit. I'll keep it simple: ```cpp #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cuda_fp16.h> // Insert val into sorted (descending) array of size k // Returns true if inserted __device__ bool insert_topk(float val, int idx, float* vals, int* indices, int k) { if (val <= vals[k-1]) return false; // Find insertion position (binary search) int lo = 0, hi = k - 1; while (lo < hi) { int mid = (lo + hi) / 2; if (val > vals[mid]) hi = mid; else lo = mid + 1; } // Shift and insert for (int i = k - 1; i > lo; i--) { vals[i] = vals[i-1]; indices[i] = indices[i-1]; } vals[lo] = val; indices[lo] = idx; return true; } ``` Let me put this together into a complete solution. Maintain local top-k (registered, sorted descending) 3. Compare each new element against local_vals[k-1] 4. If larger, binary-search insert - Warp merge: tree reduction using __shfl_down_sync - Each thread has its top-k - Pairwise merge: combine two sorted lists into one - After warp merge, lane 0 of each warp has the warp's top-k - Each warp writes its top-k to shared memory - Sync - One warp sorts the shared memory buffer, takes global top-k - Write output ``` This approach is clean and efficient. Let me implement it. Actually, for the warp merge, combining two sorted lists of k elements each into one sorted list of k elements (keeping only the largest k) can be done with a simple merge algorithm: ```cpp // Merge two sorted (descending) lists a and b, each of size k // Result goes into dst (size k), keeping only the largest k __device__ void merge_topk( const float* a, const int* a_idx, const float* b, const int* b_idx, float* dst, int* dst_idx, int k ) { int i = 0, j = 0, out = 0; while (out < k) { if (i < k && (j >= k || a[i] >= b[j])) { dst[out] = a[i]; dst_idx[out] = a_idx[i]; i++; } else { dst[out] = b[j]; dst_idx[out] = b_idx[j]; j++; } out++; } } ``` And for the warp tree reduction: ```cpp // Each thread has its local top-k in registers // Use __shfl_down_sync to pair up and merge for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { // Get partner's top-k via shuffle float partner_vals[K]; int partner_idx[K]; for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { partner_vals[i] = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_vals[i], offset); partner_idx[i] = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_idx[i], offset); } merge_topk(local_vals, local_idx, partner_vals, partner_idx, local_vals, local_idx, k); } ``` Wait, but `__shfl_down_sync` for arrays requires multiple calls. For k=64, that's 128 shuffle calls per merge round, and 5 rounds (32→16→8→4→2→1) = 640 shuffle calls. That's a lot but shuffles are fast (1 cycle each on recent hardware). Each stage is N/2 parallel comparisons. With 256 threads: - 105 * 16384/2 / 256 = 105 * 32 = 3360 comparisons per thread That's a lot. Continue → 32, 16, 8, 4, 2, 1 thread with top-64 This requires 8 merge rounds (256→128→64→32→16→8→4→2→1). Each merge round combines two sorted lists of k elements. For k=64, that's about 512 comparisons total, which is very fast. The catch: other threads are idle during the merge. But since the merge is a tiny fraction of total work (the streaming pass does n * (comparison) work), this is fine. Let me implement this approach: ``` Phase 1 (all threads, parallel): - Each thread processes n/BLOCK elements - Each thread maintains top-k in registers - Each thread writes its top-k to shared memory at offset [tid * k] Phase 2 (log reduction): - for stride = BLOCK/2, BLOCK/4, ..., 1: - if tid < stride: merge top-k from pos [tid*k] and [(tid+stride)*k] into pos [tid*k] - __syncthreads() Phase 3 (thread 0): - Write final top-k from shared[0:k] to global memory ``` This is clean and efficient. The merge phase uses O(log BLOCK * k) shared memory operations, which is tiny compared to the streaming phase. For efficient implementation of the merge, I can write a small `__device__` function that merges two sorted (descending) arrays: ```cpp __device__ void merge_two_sorted( const float* a_vals, const int* a_idx, const float* b_vals, const int* b_idx, float* out_vals, int* out_idx, int k ) { int ia = 0, ib = 0, io = 0; while (io < k) { bool take_a = (ia < k) && (ib >= k || a_vals[ia] >= b_vals[ib]); out_vals[io] = take_a ? a_vals[ia] : b_vals[ib]; out_idx[io] = take_a ? a_idx[ia] : b_idx[ib]; ia += take_a; ib += !take_a; io++; } } ``` Now, for the streaming phase, each thread loads elements from global memory. +"""Top-k via streaming CUDA kernel with warp-shuffle merge.
+
+Strategy:
+  - One thread block per batch element (or multiple chunks per row for large n).
+  - Each thread streams through its slice, maintaining a sorted top-k in registers.
+  - Within-warp reduction via __shfl_down_sync.
+  - Cross-warp merge via shared memory.
+  - For large n with batch=1, use multiple chunks + a merge pass.
+"""
+
+import math
+import os
+from typing import List
+
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+import torch.utils.cpp_extension
+
+OP_TYPE = "topk"
+SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["fp32"]
+HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# CUDA kernel source
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+_kernel_src = """
+#include <cuda_runtime.h>
+#include <cstdint>
+#include <cfloat>
+
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Device helpers: sorted (descending) top-k insertion and merge
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+// Insert (val, idx) into a sorted-descending array of size k.
+// Returns true if the value was inserted.
+__device__ __forceinline__ bool insert_topk(
+    float val, int idx,
+    float* vals, int* indices, int k
+) {
+    if (val <= vals[k - 1]) return false;
+
+    // Binary search for insertion position in descending array
+    int lo = 0, hi = k - 1;
+    #pragma unroll
+    while (lo < hi) {
+        int mid = (lo + hi) >> 1;
+        if (val > vals[mid])
+            hi = mid;
+        else
+            lo = mid + 1;
+    }
+
+    // Shift tail down and insert
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int i = k - 1; i > lo; --i) {
+        vals[i] = vals[i - 1];
+        indices[i] = indices[i - 1];
+    }
+    vals[lo] = val;
+    indices[lo] = idx;
+    return true;
+}
+
+// Merge two sorted-descending lists a[0..k-1] and b[0..k-1] into dst[0..k-1],
+// keeping only the largest k elements.
+__device__ __forceinline__ void merge_two_k(
+    const float* __restrict__ a_vals, const int* __restrict__ a_idx,
+    const float* __restrict__ b_vals, const int* __restrict__ b_idx,
+    float* __restrict__ dst_vals, int* __restrict__ dst_idx,
+    int k
+) {
+    int ia = 0, ib = 0, io = 0;
+    while (io < k) {
+        bool take_a;
+        if (ia >= k)      take_a = false;
+        else if (ib >= k) take_a = true;
+        else              take_a = (a_vals[ia] >= b_vals[ib]);
+        dst_vals[io] = take_a ? a_vals[ia] : b_vals[ib];
+        dst_idx[io] = take_a ? a_idx[ia] : b_idx[ib];
+        ia += take_a ? 1 : 0;
+        ib += take_a ? 0 : 1;
+        io++;
+    }
+}
+
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Kernel: streaming top-k with warp-shuffle + shared-memory reduction
+//
+// Grid:   (batch * num_chunks,)
+// Block:  N_THREADS (must be multiple of 32)
+//
+// Each block processes 'chunk_size' elements of one row, producing a sorted
+// top-k result in global memory.
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+template <int K, int N_THREADS, int N_WARPS>
+__global__ void topk_stream_kernel(
+    const float* __restrict__ x,      // (batch, n)
+    float* __restrict__ out_vals,     // (batch * num_chunks, K)
+    int64_t* __restrict__ out_idx,    // (batch * num_chunks, K)
+    int n,
+    int chunk_size,
+    int n_chunks_per_row
+) {
+    int global_bid = blockIdx.x;
+    int batch_idx = global_bid / n_chunks_per_row;
+    int chunk_idx = global_bid % n_chunks_per_row;
+    int tid = threadIdx.x;
+
+    const float* row_ptr = x + (int64_t)batch_idx * n;
+    int start = chunk_idx * chunk_size;
+    int end = min(start + chunk_size, n);
+    if (start >= end) return; // empty chunk (shouldn't happen)
+
+    // ---- Phase 1: streaming with per-thread local top-k ----
+    float local_vals[K];
+    int   local_idx[K];
+
+    // Init to -inf
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int i = 0; i < K; ++i) {
+        local_vals[i] = -CUDART_INF_F;
+        local_idx[i] = -1;
+    }
+
+    // Each thread processes its strided slice
+    for (int pos = start + tid; pos < end; pos += N_THREADS) {
+        float v = row_ptr[pos];
+        // Only try to insert — the function checks the threshold
+        insert_topk(v, pos, local_vals, local_idx, K);
+    }
+
+    // ---- Phase 2: within-warp reduction via __shfl_down_sync ----
+    // Tree reduction: merge pairs of threads within each warp
+    const int warp_id = tid / 32;
+    const int lane_id = tid & 31;
+    unsigned mask = __activemask();
+
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
+        // Shuffle partner's top-k values and indices
+        float partner_vals[K];
+        int   partner_idx[K];
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int i = 0; i < K; ++i) {
+            partner_vals[i] = __shfl_down_sync(mask, local_vals[i], offset);
+            partner_idx[i] = __shfl_down_sync(mask, local_idx[i], offset);
+        }
+        // Merge if we're the receiving lane
+        if (lane_id < offset) {
+            float merged_vals[K];
+            int   merged_idx[K];
+            merge_two_k(local_vals, local_idx, partner_vals, partner_idx,
+                        merged_vals, merged_idx, K);
+            #pragma unroll
+            for (int i = 0; i < K; ++i) {
+                local_vals[i] = merged_vals[i];
+                local_idx[i] = merged_idx[i];
+            }
+        }
+    }
+
+    // After warp reduction, lane 0 of each warp has the warp's top-k
+    // ---- Phase 3: cross-warp reduction via shared memory ----
+    __shared__ float smem_vals[N_WARPS * K];
+    __shared__ int   smem_idx[N_WARPS * K];
+
+    if (lane_id == 0) {
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int i = 0; i < K; ++i) {
+            smem_vals[warp_id * K

... (17194 more chars)

Also, the `__shfl_down_sync` requires that all lanes in the mask participate, but in my code, I'm using `if (lane_id < offset)` to make only some lanes merge, which would cause divergence in the shuffle. Actually, `__shfl_down_sync` reads values from all participating lanes — the `lane_id < offset` check is only to determine which lanes store the merged result. But I need to be careful: a lane that doesn't receive a merge might still need to participate in the shuffle. Actually, `__shfl_down_sync` requires all lanes in the mask to participate. If some lanes take a different branch, they still participate in the shuffle. The `lane_id < offset` check determines whether the lane's merge result is used, but the shuffle itself happens for all lanes. Wait, but `__shfl_down_sync` with a mask of `__activemask()` should be fine even with divergence, as long as all lanes execute the shuffle. The if-statement around the merge (`if (lane_id < offset)`) only affects what happens after the shuffle — lanes >= offset still executed the shuffle. Hmm, actually there's a subtle issue: with `__shfl_down_sync`, the sender is `lane_id + offset` and the receiver is `lane_id`. This means lanes `32-offset` to `31` have no valid sender (out of bounds). The behavior for out-of-bounds lanes is that they receive their own value. So for lanes with `lane_id + offset >= 32`, they get their own data back. My code handles this by only having `lane_id < offset` lanes do the merge. But there's another issue: after the first level (offset=16), some lanes have stale data in `local_vals` (because they didn't merge). In the next level (offset=8), those lanes still have their original top-k, which is fine because they'll participate as senders for offset=8. Wait, actually let me re-think. After offset=16: - Lanes 0-15: have merged top-32-16 = merged top-32 (combined from 32 threads) Wait, no. Lane 0 merged lane 0 and lane 16 → lane 0 now has top-k of lanes 0 and 16 Lane 1 merged lane 1 and lane 17 → lane 1 now has top-k of lanes 1 and 17 ... Lane 15 merged lane 15 and lane 31 → lane 15 now has top-k of lanes 15 and 31 Lanes 16-31: still have their original top-k (not merged) Then offset=8: - Lanes 0-7: merge lane i with lane i+8 - Lane 0: merges its pairs {0,16} with lane 8's pairs {8,24} - ... - Lane 7: merges its pairs {7,23} with lane 15's pairs {15,31} - Lanes 8-31: still have old data, but only lanes 0-7 receive This is correct! After all levels, lane 0 has the merged top-k of all 32 lanes. OK, the warp reduction logic is correct. Let me just fix the compilation issues and re-test. Actually, the main problem is the `#pragma unroll` on a while loop. Let me use a fixed number of iterations. reasoning (2261 chars)
The issue is that `extern "C"` in a `.cu` file doesn't automatically make the functions visible to the `.cpp` file that pybind11 generates. The `.cpp` file is what calls `PYBIND11_MODULE` and declares the functions. With `extern "C"`, the function names get C linkage, but the `.cpp` file that includes the generated header needs to see `extern "C"` declarations too. The problem is: torch's `load_inline` takes the CUDA source and generates a `.cpp` wrapper that tries to call the functions. But since the functions are in the `.cu` file with `extern "C"` and the `.cpp` file also needs to see them as `extern "C"`. Let me check how `load_inline` works. The `cpp_sources` parameter is for C++ sources, and `cuda_sources` is for CUDA sources. The generated main.cpp has the pybind11 module definition. The key is: the functions declared in `cuda_sources` need to be visible to `main.cpp`. The way `load_inline` works: 1. It takes `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources` 2. For the CUDA sources, it compiles them with nvcc 3. For the C++ sources, it compiles them with g++ 4. It generates a `main.cpp` that has the `PYBIND11_MODULE` definition, which calls `m.def(...)` for each function name in the `functions` list The issue is that `main.cpp` doesn't know about the functions unless they're declared. If I put `extern "C"` in the CUDA source, the C++ compiler still needs to see the declaration. The solution is to use `cpp_sources` to provide the declarations. Let me put the `extern "C"` declarations in `cpp_sources`: ```python _cpp_src = """ #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cstdint> extern "C" { void launch_topk_stream_64(const float*, float*, int64_t*, int, int, int, int, cudaStream_t); void launch_topk_stream_32(const float*, float*, int64_t*, int, int, int, int, cudaStream_t); void launch_topk_stream_16(const float*, float*, int64_t*, int, int, int, int, cudaStream_t); void launch_topk_stream_8(const float*, float*, int64_t*, int, int, int, int, cudaStream_t); void launch_topk_stream_1(const float*, float*, int64_t*, int, int, int, int, cudaStream_t); void launch_topk_merge_64_64(const float*, const int64_t*, float*, int64_t*, int, cudaStream_t); } """ ``` And the CUDA source should keep the `extern "C"` definitions. The kernel is hanging with a tiny input. This strongly suggests a kernel-level bug — probably a deadlock from __syncthreads() or an infinite loop. Let me think about potential issues: 1. `__syncthreads()` deadlock: if different threads hit different numbers of __syncthreads(), the kernel hangs. Let me audit my syncthreads calls. 2. The `while (lo < hi)` binary search in `insert_topk`: if somehow `lo` never reaches `hi`, this loops forever. For k=8, `hi = 7`. The loop reduces the range each iteration. This should always terminate. 3. The `merge_two_k` while loop: `while (io < k)`. This increments `io` each iteration, so it always terminates. Wait, let me look more carefully at the insert_topk function. With k=8 and all values initialized to -INFINITY: - First element: val > vals[7] (-inf) → true. lo=0, hi=7. Binary search: mid=3, val > -inf? Yes, hi=3. mid=1, val > -inf? Yes, hi=1. mid=0, val > -inf? Yes, hi=0. lo=0. Done. Insert at position 0. Good. That should work. Let me think about the __shfl_down_sync issue. With only 64 active elements but 128 threads, some threads process 0 elements. Their local_vals are all -inf. The shuffle should still work — shuffling -inf is fine. Actually, wait! I think I found the issue. With N_THREADS=128 and only 64 elements, threads 64-127 don't enter the loop at all. Their local_vals are all -INFINITY. This is fine. But after the warp reduction, in the cross-warp merge: - Warp 0: lane 0 has some real values - Warp 1: lane 0 has some real values - Warp 2: lane 0 has all -inf - Warp 3: lane 0 has all -inf Lane 0 of each warp writes to shared memory. Then the tree merge: - stride=2: warp 0 merges with warp 2, warp 1 merges with warp 3. Both merges have -inf on one side. This should be fine. - stride=1: warp 0 merges with warp 1. This all seems correct. Let me look for other issues. Hmm, maybe the issue is that `__syncwarp` or `__syncthreads` is needed after the loop but before the shuffle, to ensure all threads have completed the loop. The loop itself doesn't have a barrier, but within a warp, all threads executing in lockstep should complete the loop together (since each thread has the same number of iterations if chunk_size is a multiple of N_THREADS, or different by at most 1 within a warp). But wait, for chunk_size=64 and N_THREADS=128, threads within the same warp may have different iteration counts! Warp 0 (threads 0-31): all have 1 iteration (0 < 64, 1 < 64, ..., 31 < 64) Threads 32-63 in warp 1: all have 1 iteration Threads 64-95 in warp 2: all have 0 iterations Threads 96-127 in warp 3: all have 0 iterations Within each warp, all threads have the same iteration count. So there's no intra-warp divergence. Good. But what about the initial `if (start >= end) return;` check? start=0, end=64, so all threads proceed. OK, I'm stumped on what's causing the hang. Let me try a completely different approach. Instead of the warp shuffle merge, let me use a simpler but working kernel first. I'll use a two-phase approach: 1. Each thread processes its chunk and writes its local top-k to shared memory 2. A single thread merges all per-thread results But with 128 threads * 8 elements = 1024 elements, sorting 1024 elements sequentially in one thread would be slow but at least it would work and not hang. import torch
import torch.utils.cpp_extension

src = r"""
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <cstdint>

extern "C" void test_kernel_launcher(
    const float* x, float* out, int n, int64_t stream_i64
) {
    cudaStream_t stream = (cudaStream_t)(intptr_t)stream_i64;
    // Just copy first 8 elements
    cudaMemcpyAsync(out, x, 8 * sizeof(float), cudaMemcpyDeviceToDevice, stream);
}
"""

cpp_src = """
#include <cstdint>
extern "C" void test_kernel_launcher(const float*, float*, int, int64_t);
"""

mod = torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline(
    name="test_minimal",
    cpp_sources=cpp_src,
    cuda_sources=src,
    functions=["test_kernel_launcher"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "-arch=sm_120", "-std=c++17"],
    verbose=True,
)

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
stream = torch.cuda.current_stream(device)
x = torch.randn(64, dtype=torch.float32, device=device)
out = torch.zeros(64, dtype=torch.float32, device=device)
print("Before:", x[:8])
mod.test_kernel_launcher(x.data_ptr(), out.data_ptr(), 64, stream.cuda_stream)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print("After:", out[:8])
print("PASS")
EOF

After offset=16: - lane 0: merged lanes 0 and 16 → top-8 from 2 threads - lane 1: merged lanes 1 and 17 → top-8 from 2 threads - ... - lane 15: merged lanes 15 and 31 → top-8 from 2 threads - lanes 16-31: unchanged (still their original top-8) After offset=8: - lane 0: merged lane 0 and lane 8 → top-8 from 4 threads - ... - lane 7: merged lane 7 and lane 15 → top-8 from 4 threads After offset=4: - lane 0: merged lane 0 and lane 4 → top-8 from 8 threads - ... - lane 3: merged lane 3 and lane 7 → top-8 from 8 threads After offset=2: - lane 0: merged lane 0 and lane 2 → top-8 from 16 threads - lane 1: merged lane 1 and lane 3 → top-8 from 16 threads After offset=1: - lane 0: merged lane 0 and lane 1 → top-8 from 32 threads So lane 0 in warp 0 now has the top-8 from all 32 threads of warp 0. Similarly, lane 0 in warp 1 has the top-8 from all 32 threads of warp 1. Lane 0 in warp 2 and warp 3 have all -inf. Phase 3: cross-warp - Lane 0 of each warp writes to shared memory: - smem[0*8..0*8+7] = warp 0's top-8 - smem[1*8..1*8+7] = warp 1's top-8 - smem[2*8..2*8+7] = warp 2's top-8 (all -inf) - smem[3*8..3*8+7] = warp 3's top-8 (all -inf) __syncthreads() stride=2: - lane 0 of warp 0: merge smem[0*8] with smem[2*8] → smem[0*8] - lane 0 of warp 1: merge smem[1*8] with smem[3*8] → smem[1*8] __syncthreads() stride=1: - lane 0 of warp 0: merge smem[0*8] with smem[1*8] → smem[0*8] __syncthreads() Thread 0 writes smem[0*8..7] to output. This all seems correct. Let me try to find the hang. Actually, wait! I just noticed something. In the cross-warp merge: ```cpp for (int stride = N_WARPS / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (lane_id == 0 && wid < stride) { ... } __syncthreads(); } ``` The `__syncthreads()` is inside the for loop but AFTER the `if`. So for N_WARPS=4: - stride=2: __syncthreads() executes - stride=1: __syncthreads() executes All threads hit both __syncthreads() calls. This should be fine. But wait — in warp 0, lane 0 takes the `if` branch (lane_id==0 and wid==0 < 2) → does merge. In warp 2, lane 0 does NOT take the `if` branch (lane_id==0 but wid==2 >= 2). But both still hit __syncthreads(). OK this should be fine. All threads in the block hit __syncthreads() exactly the same number of times (2). Hmm, let me look at whether there might be an infinite loop somewhere. In `insert_topk`: ```cpp while (lo < hi) { int mid = (lo + hi) >> 1; if (val > vals[mid]) hi = mid; else lo = mid + 1; } ``` For k=8, initial lo=0, hi=7. Each iteration reduces the range by about half: - Iteration 1: mid=3. Either hi=3 or lo=4. - Iteration 2: mid=1 or 5. ... - Eventually lo >= hi. This should always terminate. The number of iterations is ceil(log2(hi-lo+1)) = ceil(log2(8)) = 3. In `merge_two_k`: ```cpp while (io < k) { ... io++; } ``` io starts at 0, increments each iteration until k-1. Always terminates. I really can't find the bug through code inspection. Let me try a different approach: instead of the warp shuffle merge, let me write the whole kernel using a simple shared memory approach where NO __shfl_down_sync is used at all. Each thread just writes its local top-k to shared memory, and a single thread merges everything. This avoids the shuffle and simplifies the logic dramatically. With 128 threads and k=8, we need 128*8=1024 elements in shared memory. That's 1024*(4+4) = 8 KB — easily fits. One thread merging 1024 sorted lists of 8 elements each: this is 1024*8=8192 elements. We can just heapify or bitonic sort. One thread sorting 8192 elements sequentially — actually this is a lot of work for one thread. With the bitonic sort being O(N log²N), 8192 * 13*14/2 ≈ 8192 * 91 ≈ 745K comparisons. At ~10ns per comparison, that's ~7.5ms. Way too slow! OK so I need parallelism in the merge. Let me try a different reduction strategy: **Parallel merge with shared memory:** 1. Each thread writes its local top-k to shared memory. 2. Use a parallel bitonic sort on the shared memory buffer, all threads participating. 3. Take top-k. With 128 threads and 1024 elements in shared memory: - Bitonic sort: 1024 elements, 10 stages * 11/2 ≈ 55 stages - Each stage: 1024/2=512 compare-exchange ops - 128 threads: 512/128 = 4 ops per thread per stage - Total: 55 * 4 = 220 compare-exchange ops per thread This is fast! The compare-exchange is just a comparison and conditional swap. Let me implement this simpler approach: ```cpp // Phase 1: per-thread local top-k (as before) // ... // Phase 2: write to shared memory __shared__ float smem_vals[N_THREADS * K]; __shared__ int smem_idx[N_THREADS * K]; for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { smem_vals[tid * K + i] = local_vals[i]; smem_idx[tid * K + i] = local_idx[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 3: parallel bitonic sort in shared memory int N = N_THREADS * K; // = 128 * K int n_pow2 = 1; while (n_pow2 < N) n_pow2 <<= 1; for (int stage = 2; stage <= n_pow2; stage <<= 1) { for (int step = stage >> 1; step > 0; step >>= 1) { int n_pairs = n_pow2 / 2; for (int p = tid; p < n_pairs; p += N_THREADS) { int i = p, j = p + step; bool asc = ((p / stage) & 1) == 0; // alternately ascending/descending if (i < N && j < N) { bool swap = asc ? (smem_vals[i] > smem_vals[j]) : (smem_vals[i] < smem_vals[j]); if (swap) { float tv = smem_vals[i]; smem_vals[i] = smem_vals[j]; smem_vals[j] = tv; int ti = smem_idx[i]; smem_idx[i] = smem_idx[j]; smem_idx[j] = ti; } } } __syncthreads(); } } // Phase 4: write top-k if (tid < K) { out_vals[...] = smem_vals[tid]; out_idx[...] = smem_idx[tid]; } ``` Wait, but this uses N_THREADS * K elements in shared memory. For K=64, N_THREADS=128: 128*64 = 8192 elements * 8 bytes = 65536 bytes = 64 KB. That fits in 99 KB opt-in shared memory. But for K=64, N_THREADS=128, sorting 8192 elements: - n_pow2 = 8192 (which IS a power of 2: 2^13) - 13 stages, each with up to 13 steps (total 13*14/2 = 91 stages) - 128 threads handling 4096 pairs per step → 4096/128 = 32 ops per thread per step - Total: 91 * 32 = 2912 compare-exchange ops per thread - Each compare-exchange: 2 loads + 1 compare + 2 stores (if swapped) ≈ maybe 10 instructions - Total: ~30K instructions per thread At ~1-2 GHz, that's ~15-30 μs. Add to that the streaming phase (~16 iterations per thread for chunk_size=2048). Stream: 16 loads + ~1-2 insertions per load ≈ 48-80 ops. Total ~30K + ~80 ops. The sort dominates. This might be OK for the multi-chunk case (where we sort only per-chunk results and merge), but it's a lot for the single-chunk case. Actually, for the single-chunk case, I could use the warp-shuffle approach I had before, which is much faster. The issue was with the hang, not the design. Hmm, let me think about what could cause the hang. Maybe it's related to `0xFFFFFFFF` for the shuffle mask. On some architectures, if a thread is inactive (due to divergence), `__shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, ...)` might hang. Let me use `__activemask()` instead, which returns only the mask of currently active threads. But as I analyzed before, within each warp, all threads have the same iteration count for the given chunk sizes. So all threads should be active. Actually, wait. `0xFFFFFFFF` requires ALL 32 lanes to be active. If even one lane is inactive (e.g., due to the thread exiting early or being predicated off), the shuffle hangs! With `__activemask()`, only active lanes participate. For the warp shuffle to work correctly, I need ALL lanes in the warp to be converged at the shuffle point. If they're not, `__activemask()` will have a partial mask, and the shuffle results for inactive lanes are undefined. The issue: after the streaming loop, are all 32 lanes in each warp converged? Let me check: For chunk_size=64, threads 0-63 process 1 element each, threads 64-127 process 0. Threads 0-31 (warp 0): all 1 element → converged after loop. Threads 32-63 (warp 1): all 1 element → converged. Threads 64-95 (warp 2): all 0 elements → converged immediately. Threads 96-127 (warp 3): all 0 elements → converged immediately. So all warps are converged after the loop! The shuffle with `0xFFFFFFFF` should work. But wait — what if the compiler reordered something? With `#pragma unroll` on the inner for loops, the compiler might do strange things. But for the outer streaming loop, there's no pragma, so it should be a regular loop. Let me try a completely different theory: what if the issue is with `__syncwarp()` being needed between the loop and the shuffle? Some NVIDIA docs recommend `__syncwarp()` before `__shfl_down_sync` to ensure all lanes are ready. Let me add `__syncwarp()` before the warp shuffle loop. Actually wait, let me just try using a simpler approach to test: have each thread write to shared memory and sort there. Even if it's slower, let's get correctness first.

+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Bitonic sort in shared memory (descending)
+// Helper macro-style function: all threads must participate, with
+// __syncthreads() between stages.
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+template <int N>
+__device__ void bitonic_sort_desc(__shared__ float* sv, __shared__ int* si,
+                                   int n_actual, int tid, int n_threads) {
+    int n = 1; while (n < N) n <<= 1;  // padded size
+    for (int stage = 2; stage <= n; stage <<= 1) {
+        for (int step = stage >> 1; step > 0; step >>= 1) {
+            int np = n >> 1;
+            for (int p = tid; p < np; p += n_threads) {
+                int i = p, j = p + step;
+                bool asc = ((p / stage) & 1) == 0;
+                bool sw = false;
+                if (i < n_actual && j < n_actual)
+                    sw = asc ? (sv[i] > sv[j]) : (sv[i] < sv[j]);
+                // Padded elements: treat as -inf for descending
+                else if (i < n_actual && j >= n_actual)
+                    sw = false;
+                else if (i >= n_actual && j < n_actual)
+                    sw = true;
+                if (sw) {
+                    float tv = sv[i]; sv[i] = sv[j]; sv[j] = tv;
+                    int   ti = si[i]; si[i] = si[j]; si[j] = ti;
+                }
+            }
+            __syncthreads();
+        }
+    }
+}

I need to use the opt-in shared memory configuration. Actually, the opt-in shared memory is 99 KB (101376 bytes). Let me use `cudaFuncSetAttribute` to configure the kernel to use more shared memory. Or better, I can use `__launch_bounds__` with the appropriate settings, or I can configure the kernel's shared memory with `cudaFuncSetAttribute(kernel, cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, ...)`. Actually, the simplest approach is to add `__launch_bounds__(128, 1)` to the kernel, which tells the compiler to use at most 128 threads per block and to optimize accordingly. This gives more registers and shared memory per block. But the issue is static shared memory (declared with `__shared__`), which is part of the kernel's binary. To use more than 48 KB of static shared memory, I need to opt in. On compute capability 9.0+, there's a way to do this. Let me check: `cudaFuncSetAttribute(kernel, cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, ...)` only affects DYNAMIC shared memory (extern __shared__). For static shared memory, the limit is still 48 KB unless you use opt-in. For compute capability 7.0+ (and Blackwell is 12.0), there's a mechanism to request more shared memory per block. The way to opt in for more static shared memory is to use the `cudaFuncSetAttribute` with `cudaFuncAttributePreferredSharedMemoryCarveout` or configure the carveout. Actually, on newer CUDA architectures, you can increase the maximum shared memory per block by specifying `__launch_bounds__(maxThreadsPerBlock, minBlocksPerMultiprocessor)`. import solution

    x = torch.randn(batch, n, dtype=torch.float32, device=device)
    vals, idx = model(x)
    ref_vals, _ = torch.topk(x, k=k, dim=-1, largest=True, sorted=True)
    assert vals.shape == (batch, k), f'bad shape {vals.shape} vs ({batch},{k})'

    print(f'  PASS')

122	    int chunk_idx = gid % n_chunks_per_row;
123	    int tid = threadIdx.x;

125	    int64_t row_off = (int64_t)batch_idx * n;
126	    int start = chunk_idx * chunk_size;
127	    int end = (start + chunk_size < n) ? (start + chunk_size) : n;
128	    if (start >= end) return;
129	
130	    // ---- Phase 1: streaming per-thread local top-k ----
131	    float lv[K];
132	    int   li[K];
133	    #pragma unroll
134	    for (int i = 0; i < K; ++i) {
135	        lv[i] = -INFINITY;
136	        li[i] = -1;
137	    }
138	    for (int pos = start + tid; pos < end; pos += N_THREADS) {
139	        float v = x[row_off + pos];
140	        insert_topk(v, pos, lv, li, K);
141	    }
142	
143	    // ---- Phase 2: write local top-k to shared memory ----
144	    __shared__ float sv[N_ELEM];
145	    __shared__ int   si[N_ELEM];
146	
147	    #pragma unroll
148	    for (int i = 0; i < K; ++i) {
149	        sv[tid * K + i] = lv[i];
150	        si[tid * K + i] = li[i];
151	    }
152	    __syncthreads();
153	
154	    // ---- Phase 3: bitonic sort in shared memory ----
155	    bitonic_sort_desc<N_ELEM>(sv, si, N_ELEM, tid, N_THREADS);
156	
157	    // ---- Phase 4: write top K (first K elements are the largest, descending) ----
158	    if (tid < K) {
159	        int64_t off = (int64_t)gid * K;
160	        out_vals[off + tid] = sv[tid];
161	        out_idx[off + tid] = (int64_t)si[tid];

SUCCESS! The issue is with passing `float*` or `const float*` through pybind11 — it causes stack smashing. But passing `int64_t` pointers works fine! Good news: the kernel runs without crashing! Bad news: the values are wrong (max diff 3.64). The issue is with the bitonic sort producing incorrect results. Let me debug this. For batch=64, n=8192, k=8: - K=8, NT=64, NE=512 - Each thread processes 128 elements - After streaming, each thread has its local top-8 (sorted descending) - All 64 threads write to shared memory → 512 elements - Bitonic sort of 512 elements The bitonic sort should produce a descending-sorted array. Let me check if the XOR-based comparison logic is correct. In bitonic sort, the order is determined by: - For stage size `k`, the bitonic direction for element `i` depends on bit `log2(k)` of `i` - `(i & stage) == 0` → ascending, `(i & stage) != 0` → descending Wait, I think the issue is the direction. Let me re-derive. In the standard bitonic sorting network for ASCENDING order: ``` for (k = 2; k <= N; k *= 2) for (j = k/2; j > 0; j /= 2) for (i = 0; i < N; i++) if ((i & k) == 0) // ascending: smaller first if (a[i] > a[i^j]) swap(a[i], a[i^j]) else // descending: larger first if (a[i] < a[i^j]) swap(a[i], a[i^j]) ``` This produces an ASCENDING sorted array. For DESCENDING, I need to flip the directions: - `(i & k) == 0` → descending (larger first) - `(i & k) != 0` → ascending (smaller first) My code does: ```cpp bool asc = ((i & stage) == 0); bool sw = asc ? (vi > vj) : (vi < vj); ``` So when `asc` is true (i&stage==0): sw = vi > vj → we want vi to be smaller than vj (vi > vj means we need to swap). This puts the smaller value at position i and larger at j. ASCENDING. When `asc` is false (i&stage!=0): sw = vi < vj → we want vi to be larger than vj. DESCENDING. Wait, this is the STANDARD ascending sort pattern. For descending, I need: - When ascending (i&stage==0): larger first → sw = vi < vj - When descending (i&stage!=0): smaller first → sw = vi > vj So: `sw = asc ? (vi < vj) : (vi > vj)` for descending. Let me fix this. Actually wait, let me double-check. In the standard bitonic sort for ASCENDING: - Block type 0 (i&stage==0): ascending (vi <= vj → smaller at i) - Block type 1 (i&stage==1): descending (vi >= vj → larger at i) After all stages, the array is sorted ascending. For DESCENDING output, I need to flip both: - Block type 0: descending (vi >= vj → larger at i) - Block type 1: ascending (vi <= vj → smaller at i) So: `sw = asc ? (vi < vj) : (vi > vj)` for descending. Let me fix this!

The bitonic sort in shared memory is very slow: for the decoder shape with 64 chunks, each chunk does a 4096-element bitonic sort. 4096 elements means 12 stages, each with an average of ~6 sub-stages, each with ~2048 comparisons across 64 threads. Total: ~12*6*2048/64 = ~2304 compare-exchange ops per thread per kernel. With 64 blocks doing this, that's a lot of work. And then the merge kernel sorts 4096 elements again. So the total work is dominated by the sort, not the streaming. 2. For the single-chunk shapes (batch=64,n=8192), we do a 512-element bitonic sort. With 64 threads and 64 blocks, that's manageable but still significant overhead. The bottleneck is the bitonic sort. Let me think about how to make this faster... Options: 1. **Reduce threads per block**: With 64 threads and k=8, the shared memory has 512 elements. If I use 32 threads instead, each thread processes 16 elements in the sort, which is more work. Better but still slow: - Shape 0 (1,131072,64): 0.14 ms vs torch.topk 0.041 ms — 3.4x slower - Shape 1 (64,8192,8): 0.155 ms vs torch.topk 0.037 ms — 4.2x slower - Shape 2 (32,16384,32): 0.258 ms vs torch.topk 0.038 ms — 6.8x slower - Shape 3 (16,12000,16): 0.277 ms vs torch.topk 0.037 ms — 7.5x slower - Shape 4 (128,4096,1): 0.013 ms vs torch.topk 0.015 ms — slightly faster! The argmax case is competitive. The other cases are slow. The issue is likely the per-element `insert_topk` which is called for every element. With batch=64, n=8192, NT=32, each thread processes 256 elements. For each element: load, compare with vals[k-1], branch. Most elements don't get inserted. Then the warp shuffle merge: 5 rounds, each exchanging K elements (8) = 40 shuffle ops. For the multi-chunk case (batch=1,n=131072): 64 blocks, each with 64 threads, chunk_size=2048. Each thread processes 32 elements. Then 64-thread warp-shuffle + shared memory. The per-element load is probably the bottleneck. Let me check if it's using efficient loads (full cache line). Each thread loads 4 bytes with stride NT=64. So thread 0 loads from addresses 0, 64, 128, ... Thread 1 loads from 4, 68, 132, ... These are strided accesses that don't coalesce into full 128-byte cache lines. For coalesced access, threads within a warp should access consecutive addresses. With NT=64 and 32 threads/warp, thread 0 (warp 0, lane 0) accesses 0, 64, 128, ... and thread 1 (warp 0, lane 1) accesses 1, 65, 129, ... These are NOT coalesced (adjacent threads access different cache lines). But wait, within a single iteration: threads 0-31 access addresses 0-31. That's 128 bytes — exactly one cache line! And those 128 bytes are loaded via 32 separate 4-byte loads, which get coalesced by the hardware into one or two 128-byte transactions. So the loads ARE coalesced within each iteration. But across iterations: thread 0 accesses 0, 64, 128, ... Each iteration jumps 64*4=256 bytes. This is fine for the memory controller. So the memory access pattern should be efficient. Let me think about what else could be slow... Actually, the key issue might be that `insert_topk` is called for EVERY element, even though the element is almost always rejected (val <= vals[k-1]). The function call overhead, the load of vals[k-1], and the comparison are ~3-5 instructions. But the GPU has to do this for every element. For shape 1 (64, 8192, 8) with 64 blocks * 32 threads = 2048 threads: - Total elements: 64 * 8192 = 524,288 - Each element: load + insert_topk call - `insert_topk`: load vals[k-1], compare, branch → ~4 instructions for rejection - Total: ~2M instructions for all elements At 1.8 GHz with 188 SMs, that should be very fast (~1 μs). But we're spending 155 μs. Something else is dominating. Hmm, maybe it's the warp shuffle merge. Let me analyze: - 5 shuffle rounds * K exchanges * 64 blocks = 5 * 8 * 2 (vals+idx) * 64 = 5,120 shuffle ops - Each shuffle: ~1 cycle. Total: trivial. What about register spilling? With K=8 and local arrays of 8 vals + 8 idxs = 16 registers, plus the shuffle merge temporaries, we might be around 50-60 registers per thread. With 32 threads (1 warp), that's 32*60 = 1920 registers. Way under the 65536 limit per SM. Wait, for shape 1 with NT=32, batch=64: we launch 64 blocks. 64 blocks spread across 188 SMs. Each SM gets ~0.34 blocks → but shared memory might limit us. With NS=K=8 (single warp, no shared memory needed), there's no shared memory pressure. Actually wait, for K=8, I'm using NT=32 (1 warp). The kernel has `if (NW > 1)` which is false, so no shared memory is used. The lane 0 write is direct from registers. So the kernel launches 64 blocks of 32 threads each, all reading from global memory. Each block processes one row. With only 1 warp per block, the SM occupancy is very low: 1 block * 1 warp = 32 threads out of 1536 = 2%! This is terrible for latency hiding. The GPU's memory system needs many in-flight requests to saturate bandwidth, and with only 32 threads per SM, we can't hide the ~300-500 cycle memory latency. The fix: use MORE threads per block, not fewer! With 128 or 256 threads per block, we'd have more warps to hide latency. But with more threads, the shared memory for the merge grows. With 128 threads and K=8: 128*8 = 1024 elements. 1024*8 = 8 KB. Fits in 48 KB. Let me try: - K=1: 128 threads, 1 warp... wait, 128 threads = 4 warps. Shared memory: 4*1=4 elements. Trivial. - K=8: 128 threads, 4 warps. Shared: 4*8=32 elements. Trivial. - K=16: 128 threads, 4 warps. Shared: 4*16=64 elements. Trivial. - K=32: 128 threads, 4 warps. Shared: 4*32=128 elements. Trivial. - K=64: 128 threads, 4 warps. Shared: 4*64=256 elements. Fine. With 128 threads (4 warps), occupancy is: - 128 threads per block, shared memory = 256*8 = 2048 bytes (for K=64) - 48 KB / 2 KB → lots of blocks per SM - 65536 regs / (128 * ~160 regs for K=64) = ~3 blocks per SM (register limited) - 3*128 = 384 threads per SM = 25% occupancy Much better! 25% occupancy gives 12 warps per SM, which should hide memory latency. But the warp-shuffle merge for K=64: 5 rounds * 64 exchanges * 2 = 640 shuffle ops per warp. With 4 warps, that's within the first phase. Then cross-warp: shared memory sort of 256 elements. Actually for cross-warp, I can do a tree merge instead of a full sort. With 4 warps, I can merge in 2 levels: - Level 1: warp 0+2, warp 1+3 → 2 results - Level 2: merge the 2 results → final Each merge combines 2 sorted lists of K elements. This is O(K) per level, not O(K log K) like a full sort. Let me redesign: use 128 threads (4 warps), warp-shuffle within each warp, then tree merge across warps. Actually, let me simplify even further. Instead of the complex warp-shuffle + cross-warp approach, let me use a simple approach: 1. 128 threads per block 2. Each thread processes its chunk, maintaining local top-k 3. Write local top-k to shared memory 4. Use a simple tree reduction: pairs of threads merge, then pairs of those, etc. 5. Final result written by thread 0 The tree reduction does 7 levels (128→64→32→16→8→4→2→1). At each level, pairs of threads merge their top-k lists. The merge is O(K) per pair, done sequentially by one thread. Total merge work for K=64: - Level 1 (128→64): 64 merges of 2K elements → 64 * O(K) = O(64*K) - Level 2 (64→32): 32 merges - ... - Total: 64+32+16+8+4+2+1 = 127 merges - Each merge: ~2K comparisons = 128 for K=64 - Total: ~16256 comparisons 16256 comparisons at ~10ns each = 162 μs. Way too slow! But if each merge is done in parallel by multiple threads... hmm that doesn't work for a tree reduction. Actually, the tree reduction is bad for this. The bitonic sort is O(N log²N) with N = threads*K, but with parallelism across threads, the wall-clock time is O(log²N * N/threads). For N=8192 (128*64), 128 threads: - 13 stages * 13/2 ≈ 84 sub-stages - Each sub-stage: 4096 comparisons / 128 threads = 32 per thread - Total: 84 * 32 = 2688 comparisons per thread - At ~10ns each: ~26.9 μs per block That's not great either. The shared memory bandwidth limits us. Hmm, let me try yet another approach. Since k is small, I can use a register-based tournament within each warp and then use the shuffle merge. The key insight: for small k, the merge is cheap. Let me compare approaches: 1. **Current approach (32 threads, 1 warp)**: Low occupancy, mostly memory-bound. 0.155 ms. 2. **128 threads, 4 warps, shuffle+shared merge**: Better occupancy, but cross-warp merge adds overhead. 3. **Reference torch.topk**: 0.037 ms. torch.topk is MUCH faster. Let me think about WHY. Torch's top-k implementation uses radix-select for small k (when k < n/2 and k < 512). Radix-select is O(n) work with simple histogram operations. For fp32: 1. First pass: count elements by top bits (e.g., top 11 bits → 2048 bins) 2. Compute prefix sums to find which bin contains the k-th element 3. If more than k elements in the target bin, refine with more bits 4. Collect elements from the target bin(s) and sort This is much faster than a full sort because it avoids O(n log n) comparisons. The work is O(n * passes) where passes ≈ 32/bits_per_pass. With 11 bits per pass, it's 3 passes. For our case (n=8192, k=8), torch can do this in ~3 passes = 24576 operations per row. Very fast. For my streaming approach: each element requires a comparison + branch = ~3 ops. 8192 * 3 = 24576 ops. Plus the shuffle merge. So the total compute is similar. But torch has better occupancy, vectorized loads, and no shuffle overhead. The key difference: torch uses multiple warps/blocks to process the data in parallel. For n=8192, torch might use 256 threads processing 32 elements each. The reduction is done via shared memory or atomics. Let me try using 256 threads (8 warps) for better occupancy. With 256 threads: - Each thread processes 8192/256 = 32 elements - Shared memory: 8 warps * K elements = 8*K (for K=8: 64 elements) - Cross-warp merge: tiny bitonic sort of 64 elements - Or tree merge: log2(8) = 3 levels Actually, 256 threads per block gives 8 warps. After warp-level shuffle merge (5 rounds), lane 0 of each warp writes to shared memory: 8*K elements. For K=8: 64 elements. Bitonic sort of 64 elements: 6 stages, ~21 sub-stages. But 256 threads for a single row means 256 threads reading 8192 elements = only 32 elements per thread. That's very little work per thread. For batch=64: 64 rows * 1 block per row = 64 blocks of 256 threads. 64 * 256 = 16384 threads total. 16384 / 1536 = ~10.7 SMs worth. 10.7/188 = 5.7% occupancy. Still low! The fundamental issue: the problem isn't very parallel (small batch * moderate n), so we can't fill all SMs. Let me try a different approach: use more blocks per row to increase parallelism, and merge results after. For shape 1 (64, 8192, 8): - Split each row into 4 chunks of 2048 - 64 * 4 = 256 blocks, each with 64 threads - Each block finds local top-8, then merge 4*8 = 32 elements per row This gives 256 blocks, which better utilizes 188 SMs. Let me implement this. For the merge of 32 elements into 8, we can do it in a second kernel with one block per batch element. For shape 1 (batch=64, n=8192): 256/64=4, chunk_size = 8192/4 = 2048. 4 chunks per row * 64 rows = 256 blocks. Merge: 4*8=32 elements. Let me try this.

reasoning (3026 chars)
Shape 0 got WORSE: 2.1 ms vs 0.23 ms before. The single-block approach is terrible because 256 threads on one SM can't saturate memory bandwidth at all. But the other shapes improved: - Shape 1: 0.031 ms (was 0.037) - Shape 2: 0.073 ms (same) - Shape 3: 0.039 ms (was 0.031 - slightly worse) - Shape 4: 0.015 ms (was 0.011 - slightly worse) For shape 0 (1, 131072, 64), I need to use multiple blocks to spread the memory reads across SMs. Let me go back to chunking but with a better merge strategy. Actually, the 128-chunk approach gave 0.23 ms for shape 0, which is much better than 2.1 ms but still 5x slower than torch.topk. Let me think about what torch.topk does differently for this shape... For n=131072, k=64: torch.topk dispatches to a radix-select kernel. The radix-select: 1. First pass: count histogram of top bits (e.g., top 10 bits → 1024 bins) 2. Compute prefix sum to find which bin(s) contain the k-th element 3. Second pass: within the target bin(s), scan again with more bits 4. Continue until k elements are selected Each pass reads the entire input once. With 4 passes (or maybe just 2 passes for well-distributed data), that's 2-4 * 512 KB = 1-2 MB of reads. At 1.8 TB/s, that's 0.6-1.1 μs. Plus compute for histogram: O(n) operations, cheap. My approach: one streaming pass + warp shuffle merge + cross-warp merge + chunk merge = more work than radix-select. Let me try implementing radix-select instead. It's the right algorithm for this problem. For fp32 top-k with radix-select: 1. View fp32 as uint32 (same bit pattern) 2. Handle sign bit: flip the sign bit so that the integer order matches float order 3. Most-significant-digit radix sort, but stop when we have k elements The algorithm: ``` values = x.view(dtype=uint32) values ^= (values >> 31) | 0x80000000 // flip sign bit for correct ordering // Start with all elements as candidates // Examine bits from MSB to LSB for bit_pos in [31, 30, ..., 0]: // Count how many candidates have 0/1 at this bit count_0 = count(candidates with bit=0) // 0 means "1" in original (larger) count_1 = count(candidates with bit=1) if count_0 >= k: // Top-k are all in the 0-bit bucket candidates = candidates with bit=0 else: // Take all 0-bit candidates, need (k - count_0) more from 1-bit bucket keep all 0-bit candidates k -= count_0 candidates = candidates with bit=1 ``` This is O(n * bits) = O(32n) in the worst case, but typically we narrow down quickly to far fewer bits (maybe 8-10 bits needed). The key operation is "count and filter by bit". This can be done efficiently in a CUDA kernel. Let me implement this as a multi-pass CUDA kernel. Each pass: 1. Read all (remaining) elements 2. Count how many have the current bit = 0 3. If enough, keep only those; otherwise keep 0-bits + continue with 1-bits This would be very fast — each pass is just a scan + count + filter. Let me implement this approach. It should be much closer to torch.topk's performance.

"Too many resources requested for launch" — this means one of the kernel launches exceeds a resource limit. The most likely culprit is the K=64 kernel with 1024 threads. With 1024 threads and K=64: - Registers per thread: ~140 (64 vals + 64 idxs + temporaries) - Total registers per block: 1024 * 140 = 143,360 registers - Max registers per block: 65536 143,360 > 65,536! The kernel won't launch because it needs too many registers. I need to either: 1. Reduce threads to fit in register budget 2. Optimize register usage (e.g., store less data per thread) With max 65536 regs per block and K=64 needing ~130 regs per thread: - Max threads = 65536/130 = 504 threads But I also need to consider occupancy. Let me try 256 threads for K=64: - 256 * 130 = 33,280 regs per block. Fits! - 65536/33280 = 1.97 → 1 block per SM (register limited) - But also shared memory: 8 warps * 64 = 512 elements, 512*8 = 4 KB. Fine. - 256/1536 = 17% occupancy with 1 block per SM. Shape 0 got even worse (3 ms vs the previous 0.23 ms). The issue is clear: with only 256 threads processing 131,072 elements, each thread does 512 iterations. But the real problem is that the inlined merge code within the shuffle loop creates massive register pressure, causing spilling to local memory, which is very slow. I need a different approach. Let me step back and think about what will actually work well. The fundamental constraint: for a single row with large n (131K) and moderate k (64), we need to: 1. Read all data with high bandwidth 2. Select top-k efficiently The multi-chunk approach (many blocks per row) is good for bandwidth but requires a merge. The single-block approach avoids the merge but has low bandwidth utilization. What if I use asynchronous memory copies to bring data into shared memory in tiles, then process each tile? This is like a software-managed cache. Actually, let me try a completely different approach: use torch tensor operations that aren't banned. I can use basic operations like comparison, scatter, gather, etc. For top-k with small k, I could use an iterative approach: 1. Find the max value 2. Set it to -inf 3. Repeat k times But this is O(k*n) = 64 * 131072 = 8.4M operations, mostly in torch's optimized kernels. Each max operation reads 512 KB. 64 passes * 512 KB = 32 MB. At 1.8 TB/s: ~18 μs. Plus kernel launch overhead: 64 * ~5 μs = 320 μs. Total ~340 μs. Not great. But what if I use `torch.max` (which is allowed!) on chunks? For n=131072, I can split into 64 chunks of 2048 each, find the max of each chunk, then take the top-64 from the 64 maxima. Wait, that's not correct. The top-k values aren't necessarily the maxima of non-overlapping chunks. But I could iterate: find max, mask it out, repeat. Actually, let me try a much simpler approach using PyTorch's `torch.argmax` (not banned!) and masking: ```python def topk_torch(x, k): batch, n = x.shape values = torch.zeros(batch, k, dtype=x.dtype, device=x.device) indices = torch.zeros(batch, k, dtype=torch.int64, device=x.device) work = x.clone() for i in range(k): idx = torch.argmax(work, dim=-1, keepdim=True) val = torch.gather(work, -1, idx) values[:, i:i+1] = val indices[:, i:i+1] = idx work.scatter_(-1, idx, float('-inf')) return values, indices ``` This uses torch.argmax (allowed), torch.gather (allowed), and torch.scatter_ (allowed). For k=64, this is 64 iterations. Each iteration scans the entire row. For n=131072, that's 64 * 131072 = 8.4M operations. But torch.argmax is highly optimized and should be fast. Let me estimate: torch.argmax on a 131072-element vector should take about the memory bandwidth time: 512 KB / 1800 GB/s = 0.28 μs. But kernel launch + actual implementation overhead makes it slower. Let's say 5-10 μs per argmax call. 64 calls = 320-640 μs. Hmm, that's not great. But what if I find multiple maxima at once? Or use a different iterative approach? Actually, for small k (like k=8), the iterative argmax is only 8 iterations. 8 * 10 μs = 80 μs. For the batched case with 64 rows: we'd do 64 separate argmax calls per iteration (one per row), so 8 * 64 = 512 kernel launches. That's bad. Let me try yet another approach: a custom CUDA kernel that does exactly what I want, but optimized to use fewer registers and more parallelism. For the (1, 131072, 64) case, use MULTIPLE blocks to scan the data, then a merge kernel. The key optimization: instead of each block doing a full top-64 of its chunk (which requires 64 registers per thread), have each block do a partial selection that requires fewer registers, and then merge. For example, each block could produce the top-256 from its chunk (using a heap or simple selection), and then the merge kernel finds the top-64 from the combined results. But top-256 requires even more registers! Alternative: use RADIX SELECT. Each block counts a histogram of the top bits, and global atomics determine which bin(s) have the top-k. Then a second pass selects the elements from those bins. Let me implement radix select properly. It's the right algorithm for this problem. For fp32, the format is [sign(1)] [exponent(8)] [mantissa(23)]. We can examine from the most significant bit downward. The algorithm for one row: 1. Convert all values to sortable uint32 2. Start with bit_pos = 31 (most significant bit = sign bit) 3. Count how many elements have bit=0 at position bit_pos 4. If count >= k: discard elements with bit=1, continue with bit_pos-1 5. Else: keep all elements with bit=0, set k = k - count, keep elements with bit=1, continue with bit_pos-1 6. Stop when k == 0 or we've processed all bits In practice, after ~8-10 bits, we typically narrow down to exactly k elements. The counting needs to be done across all blocks. I can use global atomics. But implementing this as a multi-pass CUDA kernel is complex because we need to: 1. Track which elements are "active" (still candidates) 2. Count bits in each pass 3. Filter elements Rather than tracking "active" elements, I can do a simpler approach: - Each pass processes ALL elements - Count how many have bit=0 at the current position - If enough, narrow down to those elements - Otherwise, keep 0-bits and continue with 1-bits This requires either: a. Multiple kernel launches (one per bit position), each reading all data b. Or a single kernel that iterates internally For approach (a), with 10 passes over 512 KB = 5 MB total reads. At 1.8 TB/s: ~2.8 μs for reads. Plus kernel launch overhead: 10 * 5 μs = 50 μs. Total ~53 μs. Competitive with torch.topk's 41 μs! But the kernel also needs to do the bit counting and element compaction, which adds overhead. Let me implement this. Actually, there's a much simpler approach that I've been overcomplicating. Let me just use TWO passes: Pass 1: Build a histogram of the top 16 bits (65536 bins). This gives us a coarse idea of where the top-k are. Pass 2: In the bin(s) containing the top-k, refine with the lower 16 bits using a bitonic sort or simple selection. For 131K elements, 65536 bins is fine. Each element contributes to exactly one bin. We can use global atomics to build the histogram (one atomicAdd per element, parallelized across blocks). Then a prefix sum on the histogram tells us which bins contain the top-k. Then a second pass extracts elements from those bins. Let me implement this as a CUDA kernel. Actually, this is getting very complex for a CUDA kernel. This suggests there's massive room for improvement if I can saturate the memory bus. The question is: can a single SM saturate the memory bus? On modern GPUs, each SM has a limited number of memory request slots. On Blackwell (sm_120), each SM can have ~32-64 outstanding memory transactions. Each transaction is 32-128 bytes. So 32 * 128 = 4096 bytes in flight per SM at maximum. With 1.8 TB/s, the bandwidth per SM (if evenly distributed) would be 1800/188 = 9.6 GB/s. At 9.6 GB/s, reading 512 KB takes 512/9600000 = 53 μs. Iterative argmax is even slower for all shapes except shape 4. The issue is clear: k iterations of argmax + mask is too expensive. Let me go back to my best approach (multi-chunk CUDA kernel with merge) but with optimizations: Best results so far: - Shape 0: 0.226 ms (multi-chunk with 128 blocks, then merge) - Shape 1: 0.031 ms - Shape 2: 0.073 ms - Shape 3: 0.031 ms - Shape 4: 0.011 ms The main problem is shape 0 (and somewhat shapes 2-3). Let me think about what makes shape 0 slow. For shape 0 (batch=1, n=131072, k=64) with the multi-chunk approach: - 128 chunks of 1024 elements each (or similar) - 128 blocks in the streaming pass - Then merge 128*64 = 8192 elements in the merge pass The streaming pass: 128 blocks, each with 256 threads, each processing 1024/256 = 4 elements. That's very fast (< 1 μs if all blocks run in parallel). The merge pass: 8192 elements in a bitonic sort. This needs 13 stages (since 2^13 = 8192), with ~91 sub-stages. Each sub-stage does 4096 comparisons with multiple threads. With 1024 threads: 4096/1024 = 4 per thread. 91 * 4 = 364 comparisons per thread. At ~5 ns each: ~1.8 μs. That's actually very fast! Wait, but the merge kernel is launched with `<<<1, 1024>>>` (1 block). The bitonic sort is within a single block. 1024 threads * 364 comparisons = ~373K ops. At 2 GHz: ~187 μs. But with parallelism, 364 comparisons per thread at ~5 ns each = ~1.8 μs. Plus __syncthreads() overhead: 91 barriers at ~20 cycles each = 1820 cycles = ~1 μs at 2 GHz. Total: ~3 μs for the merge. Hmm, but this assumes the bitonic sort is perfectly parallel. Let me recalculate: - 13 stages, total sub-stages = 13*14/2 = 91 - Each sub-stage has n/2 = 4096 compare-exchange operations - With 1024 threads: 4096/1024 = 4 ops per thread per sub-stage - Each op: load 2 values, compare, conditional swap (2 stores) - ~5-10 instructions per op → 20-40 per sub-stage - Total: 91 * 30 = 2730 instructions per thread - At ~0.5 ns per instruction (2 GHz, 2 IPC): ~1.4 μs - Plus 91 __syncthreads() at ~20 cycles each: ~1 μs - Total: ~2.4 μs That's very fast! So why is shape 0 taking 226 μs? Maybe the issue is that the merge kernel uses DYNAMIC shared memory, and the `cudaFuncSetAttribute` call is causing issues, or the shared memory allocation is slow. Or maybe the streaming pass is the bottleneck. With 128 blocks of 256 threads = 32768 threads. But only 188 SMs * 1536 threads/SM / 256 threads/block... actually with 256 threads per block, each SM can fit at most 1536/256 = 6 blocks (thread limited). But register pressure might reduce this. For K=64 with 256 threads: - 256 * ~130 regs = 33280 regs per block - 65536/33280 = 1.97 → 1 block per SM (register limited!) So only 1 block of 256 threads per SM. With 128 blocks: 128 SMs needed, of 188 available → 128 blocks run in 1 wave. But each block processes only 1024 elements (4 per thread). That's 4 loads + 4 comparisons. The kernel launch overhead dominates. Actually, the kernel launch overhead on CUDA is about 5-10 μs. Plus the merge kernel launch: another 5-10 μs. Total: 10-20 μs just for launches. Plus streaming kernel execution: maybe 10 μs. Plus merge: 3 μs. Total: ~23-33 μs. But we're measuring 226 μs. Something else is going on. Maybe the `cudaFuncSetAttribute` is expensive, or the dynamic shared memory allocation has overhead. Let me try a version without dynamic shared memory — use a fixed-size shared memory allocation in the merge kernel. For shape 0 with 128 chunks of K=64: total merge elements = 8192. But 8192 elements * 8 bytes = 64 KB > 48 KB default. I need to use fewer chunks. With 64 chunks of K=64: 4096 elements. 4096 * 8 = 32 KB. Fits in 48 KB default! So I can use chunk_size = 131072/64 = 2048 for shape 0. This gives 64 chunks, merge 4096 elements. The merge fits in default shared memory (no opt-in needed). Let me go back to the multi-chunk approach with chunk_size = 2048 for shape 0 and see if we can get better performance without the cudaFuncSetAttribute. Optimized chunk sizes For shape 0: chunk_size = 2048, 64 chunks, merge 4096 elements (fits in 32 KB shared) For shape 1: chunk_size = 4096, 2 chunks, merge 16 elements For shape 2: chunk_size = 4096, 4 chunks, merge 128 elements For shape 3: chunk_size = 3000, 4 chunks, merge 64 elements (for k=16) For shape 4: chunk_size = 4096, 1 chunk, no merge Let me also consider optimizing the kernel: use fewer threads to reduce register pressure. For K=64 with 256 threads, register pressure limits to 1 block per SM. With 128 threads: 128 * 130 = 16640 regs per block, 65536/16640 = 3.9 → 3 blocks per SM. Better! But 128 threads per block means each thread processes more elements (2048/128 = 16 per thread for shape 0). That's still fast. For the merge kernel: K=64, N_CHUNKS=64, merge 4096 elements. +
+Each block processes one chunk with warp-shuffle + shared-memory reduction.
+A merge pass combines chunk results via bitonic sort.
+Chunk sizes chosen so merge fits in 48 KB default shared memory.

+#include <cstdint>
+extern "C" {
+void launch_topk_64(int64_t,int64_t,int64_t,int,int,int,int);
+void launch_topk_32(int64_t,int64_t,int64_t,int,int,int,int);
+void launch_topk_16(int64_t,int64_t,int64_t,int,int,int,int);
+void launch_topk_8(int64_t,int64_t,int64_t,int,int,int,int);
+void launch_topk_1(int64_t,int64_t,int64_t,int,int,int,int);
+void launch_merge(int64_t,int64_t,int64_t,int64_t,int,int,int);
+}
+"""
+
+_cuda_src = r"""
+#include <cuda_runtime.h>
+#include <cstdint>
+#include <cfloat>
+#include <math.h>
+
+// ---- helpers ----
+__device__ bool insert_topk(float val, int idx, float* vals, int* indices, int k) {
+    if (val <= vals[k-1]) return false;
+    int lo=0, hi=k-1;
+    while(lo<hi){ int mid=(lo+hi)>>1; if(val>vals[mid])hi=mid; else lo=mid+1; }
+    for(int i=k-1;i>lo;--i){ vals[i]=vals[i-1]; indices[i]=indices[i-1]; }
+    vals[lo]=val; indices[lo]=idx; return true;
+}
+
+// ---- streaming kernel ----
+template <int K, int NT>
+__global__ void topk_kernel(
+    const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ ov, int64_t* __restrict__ oi,
+    int n, int cs, int nc
+) {
+    int gid=blockIdx.x, bid=gid/nc, cid=gid%nc, tid=threadIdx.x;
+    int64_t ro = (int64_t)bid*n;
+    int start=cid*cs, end=min(start+cs,n);
+    if(start>=end) return;
+
+    float lv[K]; int li[K];
+    for(int i=0;i<K;++i){ lv[i]=-INFINITY; li[i]=-1; }
+    for(int pos=start+tid; pos<end; pos+=NT) insert_topk(x[ro+pos],pos,lv,li,K);
+
+    // within-warp shuffle merge
+    const int lane=tid&31;
+    for(int off=16;off>0;off>>=1){
+        float pv[K]; int pi[K];
+        for(int i=0;i<K;++i){ pv[i]=__shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff,lv[i],off); pi[i]=__shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff,li[i],off); }
+        if(lane<off){
+            float mv[K];int mi[K]; int ia=0,ib=0,io=0;
+            while(io<K){ bool ta=(ia<K)&&(ib>=K||lv[ia]>=pv[ib]); mv[io]=ta?lv[ia]:pv[ib]; mi[io]=ta?li[ia]:pi[ib]; ia+=ta; ib+=!ta; io++; }
+            for(int i=0;i<K;++i){ lv[i]=mv[i]; li[i]=mi[i]; }
+        }
+    }
+
+    // cross-warp tree merge in shared memory
+    constexpr int NW=NT/32, NS=NW*K;
+    __shared__ float sv[NS];
+    __shared__ int   si[NS];
+    int wid=tid/32;
+    if(lane==0){ for(int i=0;i<K;++i){ sv[wid*K+i]=lv[i]; si[wid*K+i]=li[i]; } }
+    __syncthreads();
+    for(int stride=NW/2;stride>0;stride>>=1){
+        if(lane==0 && wid<stride){
+            float mv[K];int mi[K]; int ia=0,ib=0,io=0;
+            float* av=&sv[wid*K];int* ai=&si[wid*K];
+            float* bv=&sv[(wid+stride)*K];int* bi=&si[(wid+stride)*K];
+            while(io<K){ bool ta=(ia<K)&&(ib>=K||av[ia]>=bv[ib]); mv[io]=ta?av[ia]:bv[ib]; mi[io]=ta?ai[ia]:bi[ib]; ia+=ta; ib+=!ta; io++; }
+            for(int i=0;i<K;++i){ sv[wid*K+i]=mv[i]; si[wid*K+i]=mi[i]; }
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+    }
+    if(tid==0){ int64_t off=(int64_t)gid*K; for(int i=0;i<K;++i){ ov[off+i]=sv[i]; oi[off+i]=(int64_t)si[i]; } }
+}
+
+// ---- merge kernel: bitonic sort in shared memory ----
+__global__ void merge_kernel(
+    const float* __restrict__ cv, const int64_t* __restrict__ ci,
+    float* __restrict__ ov, int64_t* __restrict__ oi,
+    int nc, int k
+) {
+    int bid=blockIdx.x, tid=threadIdx.x, total=nc*k;
+    extern __shared__ char smem[];
+    float* sv=(float*)smem;
+    int*   si=(int*)(smem+total*sizeof(float));
+    for(int i=tid;i<total;i+=blockDim.x){ int64_t src=(int64_t)bid*total+i; sv[i]=cv[src]; si[i]=(int)ci[src]; }
+    __syncthreads();
+    int n2=1; while(n2<total) n2<<=1;
+    for(int stage=2;stage<=n2;stage<<=1){
+        for(int step=stage>>1;step>0;step>>=1){
+            for(int i=tid;i<n2;i+=blockDim.x){
+                int ixj=i^step; if(ixj<=i) continue;
+                float vi=(i<total)?sv[i]:-INFINITY, vj=(ixj<total)?sv[ixj]:-INFINITY;
+                bool asc=((i&stage)==0), sw=asc?(vi<vj):(vi>vj);
+                if(sw){
+                    if(i<total&&ixj<total){ float tv=sv[i];sv[i]=sv[ixj];sv[ixj]=tv; int ti=si[i];si[i]=si[ixj];si[ixj]=ti; }
+                    else if(i<total){ sv[i]=vj; si[i]=(ixj<total)?si[ixj]:-1; }
+                    else if(ixj<total){ sv[i]=vi; si[i]=(i<total)?si[i]:-1; }
+                }
+            }
+            __syncthreads();
+        }
+    }
+    for(int i=tid;i<k;i+=blockDim.x){ ov[(int64_t)bid*k+i]=sv[i]; oi[(int64_t)bid*k+i]=(int64_t)si[i]; }
+}
+
+// ---- launchers ----
+extern "C" {
+void launch_topk_64(int64_t xp,int64_t ovp,int64_t oip,int B,int n,int cs,int nc){
+    auto*x=(const float*)xp;auto*ov=(float*)ovp;auto*oi=(int64_t*)oip;
+    topk_kernel<64,256><<<B*nc,256>>>(x,ov,oi,n,cs,nc);
+}
+void launch_topk_32(int64_t xp,int64_t ovp,int64_t oip,int B,int n,int cs,int nc){
+    auto*x=(const float*)xp;auto*ov=(float*)ovp;auto*oi=(int64_t*)oip;
+    topk_kernel<32,128><<<B*nc,128>>>(x,ov,oi,n,cs,nc);
+}
+void launch_topk_16(int64_t xp,int64_t ovp,int64_t oip,int B,int n,int cs,int nc){
+    auto*x=(const float*)xp;auto*ov=(float*)ovp;auto*oi=(int64_t*)oip;
+    topk_kernel<16,128><<<B*nc,128>>>(x

These look good. The bottleneck for shape 0 is the merge (4096 elements). 4096-element bitonic sort takes ~2.5 μs (as calculated). But we're at 165 μs. The streaming kernel might be taking most of the time. With 64 blocks and 256 threads, each processes 2048/256 = 8 elements. That's 8 iterations at ~5 ops each = 40 ops. Plus warp shuffle merge (5 rounds, 64 exchanges each = 320 shuffles). Plus cross-warp merge (3 levels, merging 64 elements each). Total maybe 1000-2000 operations per thread. At ~0.5 ns per op: ~0.5-1 μs. Plus kernel launch overhead: ~5-10 μs. Total: ~6-11 μs per kernel launch. Two launches: ~12-22 μs. But we're measuring 165 μs. Something is much slower than expected. Wait — maybe the issue is the register spilling in the merge function. The inlined `merge_two` function within the shuffle loop creates register pressure. With K=64, each thread has lv[64] and li[64] (128 registers), plus the shuffle merge temporaries pv[64], pi[64] (another 128 registers), plus merge temporary mv[64], mi[64] (yet another 128). That's 384 registers! On Blackwell with 65536 registers per SM, with 256 threads: 65536/256 = 256 regs/thread max. At 384, we get massive spilling to local memory. The compiler might optimize by reusing registers, but the shuffle loop requires lv, pv, and mv to all be live simultaneously. That's 192+ registers for the vals alone (lv, pv, mv are all float arrays of size K=64). This spilling to local memory is killing performance. Each spilled access goes to L1/L2 cache (or worse, DRAM). For 320 shuffle rounds with 128 registers spilled per round, that's 40960 spilled loads/stores per thread. At ~100 cycles each for L1 access: ~4M cycles = 2.2 ms at 2 GHz. Wow, that's the bottleneck! Solution: use a separate, non-inlined function for the warp merge, or use shared memory for the merge instead of registers. Let me try using shared memory for the merge: each warp writes its top-K to shared memory, then synchronizes, and a designated thread merges pairs. Actually, a cleaner solution: instead of the 5-round shuffle merge within the warp, have all 32 threads in the warp write their top-K to shared memory, then use shared memory bitonic sort to merge within the warp. This avoids register spilling. With 32 threads per warp * K elements: for K=64, 32*64 = 2048 elements. Bitonic sort of 2048: 11 stages, ~66 sub-stages, with 32 threads → 66*1024/32 = 2112 compare-exchange per thread. Each compare-exchange: 2 loads + compare + 2 conditional stores = ~5-10 ops. Total: 21K ops. At ~0.5 ns/op: ~10.6 μs per block. That's too much for a per-block operation. Better approach: within-warp merge can be done differently. Instead of 5 rounds of pairwise shuffle-merges, use a single shared-memory merge within the warp, but optimized. Actually, the SIMPLEST fix: don't inline the merge. Put it in a separate `__device__ __noinline__` function. This might reduce register pressure because the compiler can spill the caller's registers to the stack before calling the merge function. Or, restructure the merge to use less register pressure: - Instead of shuffling all K vals and K idxs, shuffle one at a time - Process the merge incrementally Let me try the simplest fix first: `__noinline__` on the merge function. Actually, another approach: within-warp reduction can be done by having threads cooperate. Instead of lane 0 absorbing everything, use a tournament where each round eliminates half the threads. Let me try a completely different within-warp reduction method: ```cpp // Within-warp reduction using shared memory (avoids register spilling) __shared__ float ws[32 * K]; // 32 threads * K elements per warp __shared__ int wi[32 * K]; // Each thread writes its top-K to shared memory for(int i=0;i<K;++i){ ws[lane*K+i]=lv[i]; wi[lane*K+i]=li[i]; } __syncwarp(); // or __syncthreads() for cross-warp safety // Bitonic sort within the warp's data in shared memory // ... only 32*K elements instead of NT*K ``` But this requires 32*64=2048 elements in shared memory per warp. With NW warps, total shared = NW*2048*8 bytes. For NW=8 (256 threads): 8*2048*8 = 131 KB. EXCEEDS 48 KB! So I can't use this approach for all warps simultaneously. What if I do one warp at a time? Warp 0 processes its data, then warp 1, etc. But that's sequential and slow. OK, let me try yet another approach. For the within-warp reduction, use a register-friendly method: ```cpp // Instead of shuffling all K elements at once, do it iteratively for(int off=16; off>0; off>>=1) { // Each round: we merge K-element lists // But we don't need to shuffle all elements at once // Shuffle one pair of values at a time, merge into temporary ... } ``` This is what I'm already doing. The issue is that the compiler needs to keep lv, pv, and mv all live. With `__noinline__` on the merge function, the compiler might spill lv to the stack before calling merge, reducing register pressure. Let me try adding `__noinline__` to the merge function. But actually, the merge is already inlined in the current code (it's a while loop inside the shuffle loop, not a separate function). Let me restructure: ```cpp // Per-warp merge helper: called only by active lanes template<int K> __device__ __noinline__ void merge_lists(float* a, int* ai, float* b, int* bi, float* dst, int* dsti) { int ia=0,ib=0,io=0; while(io<K){ bool ta=(ia<K)&&(ib>=K||a[ia]>=b[ib]); dst[io]=ta?a[ia]:b[ib]; dsti[io]=ta?ai[ia]:bi[ib]; ia+=ta; ib+=!ta; io++; } } ``` By making it `__noinline__`, the caller doesn't need to keep all three arrays (a, b, dst) live simultaneously in registers. The compiler can spill the caller's lv to the stack, call merge_lists with lv and pv as inputs and mv as output, and then copy mv back to lv. But wait, the caller still has lv[K] and pv[K] which are 2*64=128 values. That's less than 384. Let me try this approach.

The key metric is peak_fraction (bandwidth utilization) Looking at the bandwidth numbers: - Shape 0: 2.6 GB/s out of 1800 GB/s = 0.15% - Shape 1: 54 GB/s out of 1800 GB/s = 3% - Shape 4: 206 GB/s out of 1800 GB/s = 11% These are all well below the 10% threshold needed for `peak_fraction >= 0.1`.
